<TABLE>
<TR><TD COLSPAN=5><a name="description"></a>&nbsp;<p><p align='center'><strong>2.A.7 The Drug/Metabolite Transporter (DMT) Superfamily</strong></p>
<p>The DMT Superfamily consists of 44 recognized families, each, in general, with a characteristic function, size and topology (<span id='ref2774.11432728' class='cite'>Jack <em>et al.</em>, 2001</span>; <span id='ref8612.21569384' class='cite'>V&auml;stermark <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>). These phylogenetic families will be presented and described below; references, when available, will be provided, and representative well-characterized proteins, when available, will be tabulated. Lolkema et al. (<span id='ref5150.18384811' class='cite'>2008</span>) have presented bioinformatic analysis of prokaryotic members of the DMT (DUF606) superfamily concerning evolution of the antiparallel arrangements of the two homologous 5TMS domains. The primordial SMR-type permeases may have resulted from duplication of a 2 TMS-encoding genetic element, which added one TMS to give five TMSs, and then duplicated to give 10 TMS proteins: 2 &rarr; 4 &rarr; 5 &rarr; 10 (TMSs), (<span id='ref7679.21194372' class='cite'>Lam <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>). One member of the SMR-2 family (2.A.7.22.2) is a lipid (isoprenoid) flippase (<span id='ref7001.17928292' class='cite'>Yan <em>et al.</em>, 2007</span>; <span id='ref7000.20043909' class='cite'>Contreras <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>).&nbsp; Most nucleotide-sugar transporter in the endoplasmic reticulum and Golgi of eukaryotic cells are members of the DMT superfamily (<span id='ref10647.23506892' class='cite'>Song 2013</span>). Nucleotide-sugar transport into the Mammalian Golgi has been reviewed (<span id='ref34555221.35955785' class='cite'>Maszczak-Seneczko <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). DMT porters have the DMT fold (<span id='ref34555317.36164651' class='cite'>Ferrada and Superti-Furga 2022</span>).</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>2.A.7.1 The 4 TMS Small Multidrug Resistance (SMR) Family</strong></p>
<p>SMR family pumps are prokaryotic transport systems consisting of homodimeric or heterodimeric structures (<span id='ref5619.11378963' class='cite'>Chung and Saier, 2001</span>; <span id='ref4405.17942072' class='cite'>Bay et al., 2007</span>; <span id='ref11376.19549332' class='cite'>Bay and Turner 2009</span>). The subunits of these systems are of 100-120 amino acid residues in length and span the membrane as &alpha;-helices four times. Functionally characterized members of the SMR family catalyze multidrug efflux driven drug:H<sup>+</sup> antiport where the proton motive force provides the driving force for drug efflux. The drugs transported are generally cationic, and a simple cation antiport mechanism involving the conserved Glu-14 has been proposed (<span id='ref5581.0' class='cite'>Yerushalmi and Schuldiner, 2000</span>). This mechanism suggests a requisite, mutually exclusive occupancy of Glu-14, providing a simple explanation for coupling the movement of two positively charged molecules. One system (YdgEF of <em>E. coli</em>; TC# 2.A.7.1.8) is reported to confer resistance to anionic detergents (<span id='ref5618.11566977' class='cite'>Nishino and Yamaguchi, 2001</span>). See 2.A.7.1.1 for substrates transporter by SMR systems (<span id='ref34557107.37645731' class='cite'>Lucero <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p>
<p>The 3-D structure of the dimeric EmrE shows opposite orientation of the two subunits in the membrane (<span id='ref6724.18024586' class='cite'>Chen <em>et al.</em>, 2007</span>). The first three transmembrane helices from each monomer surround the substrate binding chamber, whereas the fourth helices participate only in dimer formation. Selenomethionine markers clearly indicate an antiparallel orientation for the monomers, supporting a 'dual topology' model. On the basis of available structural data, a model for the proton-dependent drug efflux mechanism of EmrE was proposed. Interestingly, <span id='ref6979.20308069' class='cite'>Nasie <em>et al.</em>, (2010)</span> suggest that EmrE can insert randomly in two&nbsp;orientations and can exhibit activity in both the parallel and antiparallel orientations. The orientation of small multidrug resistance transporter subunits in the membrane correlate with the positive-inside rule (<span id='ref7680.20643145' class='cite'>Kolbusz <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>). A comprehensive review of the classes of efflux pump inhibitors from 
various sources, highlighting their structure-activity relationships, 
which can be useful for medicinal chemists in the pursuit of novel 
efflux pump inhibitors, has appeared (<span id='ref34549604.29424299' class='cite'>Dur&atilde;es <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).&nbsp; Drug metabolising enzymes and transporters in the paediatric duodenum have been quantitated (<span id='ref34557382.37625656' class='cite'>Goelen <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp; Members of the SMRGdx subtype can export the degradation products of 
metformin, helping bacteria adapt to high environmental levels of the 
commonly prescribed diabetes medication (<span id='ref34557776.38324209' class='cite'>Short 2024</span>).</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>2.A.7.2 The 5 TMS Bacterial/Archaeal Transporter (BAT) Family</strong></p>
<p>The BAT family consists of 5 TMS proteins from bacteria and archaea.  None of these proteins is functionally characterized.</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>2.A.7.3 The 10 TMS Drug/Metabolite Exporter (DME) Family</strong></p>
<p>The DME family is a large family of integral membrane proteins with sizes ranging from 287 to 310 amino acyl residues and exhibiting 10 putative &alpha;-helical transmembrane spanners (TMSs).  These proteins are derived from phylogenetically divergent bacteria and archaea, and <em>B. subtilis, E. coli, S. coelicolor</em> and <em>A. fulgidus</em> have multiple paralogues.  Distant eukaryotic homologues are more closely related to DME family members than to other DM superfamily members can be found (i.e., the Riken gene product of the mouse (BAC31006)).</p>
<p>Proteins of the DME family evidently arose by an internal gene duplication event as the first halves of these proteins are homologous to the second halves.  One of these prokaryotic proteins, YdeD, is functionally characterized and exports cysteine metabolites in <em>E. coli.</em> Another, RhtA of <em>E. coli,</em> exports threonine and homoserine.  A third, Sam of <em>Rickettsia prowazekii,</em> takes up S-adenosylmethionine (TC #3.A.7.3.7; <span id='ref5625.0' class='cite'>Tucker <em>et al</em>., 2003</span>).  In addition, several members of the DME family have been implicated in solute transport.  Thus, the MttP protein of the archaeon, <em>Methanosarcina barkeri,</em> may transport methylamine (<span id='ref5584.9006042' class='cite'>Ferguson and Krzycki, 1997</span>); MadN is encoded within the malonate utilization operon of <em>Malonomonas rubra</em> and may be an acetate efflux pump, and PecM is encoded within a locus of <em>Erwinia chrysanthemi</em> controlling pectinase, cellulase and blue pigment production and might export the pigment indigoidine, produced by gene products encoded in the pecM operon.  The PecM protein has been  shown experimentally to exhibit a 10 TMS topology (<span id='ref5586.11321588' class='cite'>Rouanet and Nasser, 2001</span>).</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>2.A.7.4 The Plant Drug/Metabolite Exporter (P-DME) Family</strong></p>
<p><span style='caret-color: #000000; color: #000000;'>The P-DME 
(UMAMIT) family is a large subset of the DME family. All of these 
proteins are derived from plants, and they cluster loosely together on a
 phylogenetic tree that includes all members of the DME and P-DME 
families. All of these proteins are predicted by various methods to have
 10 TMSs. If this suggestion proves to be correct, then the two halves 
of these proteins will have opposite orientation in the membrane. The 
P-DME family members have been called UMAMIT, and form large gene 
families in <em>Arabidopsis</em> (47 members) and rice (53 members). The few characterized members from <em>Arabidopsis </em>mediate
 amino acid export from the cytosol, with two of them shown to function 
as facilitators. They can be found in the plasma membrane (Ladwig et al 
2012, Muller et al 2015, Besnard et al 2016, 2018) or the vacuolar 
membrane (Ranocha et al, 2010, Besnard et al 2018). They play multiple 
role in amino acid translocation between the organs of the plants, e.g. 
from leaves to seeds or to roots&rdquo;.</span></p>
<hr />
<p><strong>2.A.7.5 The Glucose/Ribose Porter (GRP) Family</strong></p>
<p>The glucose/ribose uptake (GRU) family includes two functionally characterized members, a glucose uptake permease of <em>Staphylococcus xylosus,</em> and a probable ribose uptake permease of <em>Lactobacillus sakei.</em> Both proteins probably function by H<sup>+</sup> symport.</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>2.A.7.6 The L-Rhamnose Transporter (RhaT) Family</strong></p>
<p>The RhaT family includes only 2 proteins, the rhamnose:H<sup>+</sup> symporters of <em>E. coli</em> and <em>Salmonella typhimurium,</em> both of which have been functionally characterized.  The RhaT proteins of both species are 344 aas long with 10 putative TMSs.</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>2.A.7.7 The Chloramphenicol-Sensitivity Protein (RarD) Family</strong></p>
<p>No member of the RarD family is functionally characterized.  Members of the family are from Gram-negative bacteria, Gram-positive bacteria and possibly archaea.  They vary in size from 250-300 residues.  They exhibit 10 TMSs.</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>2.A.7.8 The <em>Caenorhabditis elegans</em> ORF (CEO) Family</strong></p>
<p>The CEO family is a small family of 6 paralogues encoded within the genome of <em>C. elegans.</em> None of these proteins is functionally characterized.</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>2.A.7.9 The Triose-phosphate Transporter (TPT) Family</strong></p>
<p>Functionally characterized members of the former TPT family are derived from the inner envelope membranes of chloroplasts and nongreen plastids of plants.  However, homologues are also present in yeast.  <em>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</em> has three functionally uncharacterized TPT paralogues encoded within its genome.  Under normal physiological conditions, chloroplast TPTs mediate a strict antiport of substrates, frequently exchanging an organic three carbon compound phosphate ester for inorganic phosphate (P<sub>i</sub>). Normally, a triose-phosphate, 3-phosphoglycerate, or another phosphorylated C<sub>3</sub> compound made in the chloroplast during photosynthesis, exits the organelle into the cytoplasm of the plant cell in exchange for P<sub>i</sub>.   These transporters are members of a subfamily, the TPT subfamily within the TPT family.  Experiments with reconstituted translocators in artificial membranes indicate that transport can also occur by a channel-like uniport mechanism with up to 10-fold higher transport rates.  Channel opening may be induced by a membrane potential of large magnitude and/or by high substrate concentrations.  Nongreen plastid and chloroplast carriers, such as those from maize endosperm and root membranes, mediate transport of C<sub>3</sub> compounds phosphorylated at carbon atom 2, particularly phosphoenolpyruvate, in exchange for P<sub>i</sub>.  These are the phosphoenolpyruvate:P<sub>i</sub> antiporters (the PPT subfamily).   Glucose-6-P has also been shown to be a substrate of some plastid translocators (the GPT subfamily).  These three subfamilies of proteins (TPT, PPT and GPT) are divergent in sequence as well as substrate specificity, but their substrate specificities overlap.</p>
<p>Each TPT family protein consists of about 400-450 amino acyl residues with 5-8 putative transmembrane &alpha;-helical spanners TMSs).  The actual number has been proposed to be 6 for the plant proteins as for mitochondrial carriers (TC# 2.A.29) and members of several other transporter families.   However, proteins of the TPT family do not exhibit significant sequence similarity with the latter proteins, and there is no evidence for an internal repeat sequence.  TPT proteins may exist as homodimers in the membrane.</p>
<p>The generalized reaction catalyzed by the proteins of the TPT family is:</p>
<p align='center'>organic phosphate ester (in) + P<sub>i</sub> (out) ⇌&nbsp;organic phosphate ester (out) + P<sub>i</sub> (in).</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>2.A.7.10 The UDP-N-Acetylglucosamine:UMP Antiporter (UAA) Family</strong></p>
<p>Nucleotide-sugar transporters (NSTs) are found in the Golgi apparatus and the endoplasmic reticulum of eukaryotic cells.  Members of the family have been sequenced from yeast, protozoans and animals.  Animals such as <em>C. elegans</em> possess many of these transporters.  Humans have at least two closely related isoforms of the UDP-galactose:UMP exchange transporter.</p>
<p>NSTs generally appear to function by antiport mechanisms, exchanging a nucleotide-sugar for a nucleotide.   Thus, CMP-sialic acid is exchanged for CMP; GDP-mannose is preferentially exchanged for GMP, and UDP-galactose and UDP-N-acetylglucosamine are exchanged for UMP (or possibly UDP).   Other nucleotide sugars (e.g., GDP-fucose, UDP-xylose, UDP-glucose, UDP-N-acetylgalactosamine, etc.) may also be transported in exchange for various nucleotides, but their transporters have not been molecularly characterized.  Each compound appears to be translocated by its own transport protein.  Transport allows the compound, synthesized in the cytoplasm, to be exported to the lumen of the Golgi apparatus or the endoplasmic reticulum where it is used for the synthesis of glycoproteins and glycolipids.  Comparable transport proteins exist for ATP which phosphorylates proteins, and phosphoadenosine phosphosulfate (PAPS) which is used as a percursor for protein sulfation.  It is not known if these transport proteins are members of the DMT superfamily.</p>
<p>The sequenced NSTs are generally of about 320-340 amino acyl residues in length and exhibit 8-12 putative transmembrane &alpha;-helical spanners.  An 8 TMS model has been presented by Kawakita et al. (<span id='ref5656.9562605' class='cite'>1998</span>) for the human UDP galactose transporter 1.</p>
<p>The generalized reaction catalyzed by NSTs is:</p>
<p align='center'>nucleotide-sugar (cytoplasm) + nucleotide (lumen) ⇌&nbsp;nucleotide-sugar (lumen) + nucleotide (cytoplasm)</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>2.A.7.11 The UDP-Galactose:UMP Antiporter (UGA) Family</strong></p>
<p>Nucleotide-sugar transporters (NSTs) are found in the Golgi apparatus and the endoplasmic reticulum of eukaryotic cells.  Members of the family have been sequenced from yeast, protozoans and animals.  Animals such as <em>C. elegans</em> possess many of these transporters.  Humans have at least two closely related isoforms of the UDP-galactose:UMP exchange transporter.</p>
<p>NSTs generally appear to function by antiport mechanisms, exchanging a nucleotide-sugar for a nucleotide.   Thus, CMP-sialic acid is exchanged for CMP; GDP-mannose is preferentially exchanged for GMP, and UDP-galactose and UDP-N-acetylglucosamine are exchanged for UMP (or possibly UDP).   Other nucleotide sugars (e.g., GDP-fucose, UDP-xylose, UDP-glucose, UDP-N-acetylgalactosamine, etc.) may also be transported in exchange for various nucleotides, but their transporters have not been molecularly characterized.  Each compound appears to be translocated by its own transport protein.  Transport allows the compound, synthesized in the cytoplasm, to be exported to the lumen of the Golgi apparatus or the endoplasmic reticulum where it is used for the synthesis of glycoproteins and glycolipids.  Comparable transport proteins exist for ATP which phosphorylates proteins, and phosphoadenosine phosphosulfate (PAPS) which is used as a percursor  for protein sulfation.  It is not known if these transport proteins are members of the DMT superfamily.</p>
<p>The sequenced NSTs are generally of about 320-340 amino acyl residues in length and exhibit 8-12 putative transmembrane &alpha;-helical spanners.  An 8 TMS model has been presented by Kawakita et al. (<span id='ref5656.9562605' class='cite'>1998</span>) for the human UDP galactose transporter 1.</p>
<p>The generalized reaction catalyzed by NSTs is:</p>
<p align='center'>nucleotide-sugar (cytoplasm) + nucleotide (lumen) ⇌&nbsp;nucleotide-sugar (lumen) + nucleotide (cytoplasm)</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>2.A.7.12 The CMP-Sialate:CMP Antiporter (CSA) Family</strong></p>
<p>Nucleotide-sugar transporters (NSTs) are found in the Golgi apparatus and the endoplasmic reticulum of eukaryotic cells.  Members of the family have been sequenced from yeast, protozoans and animals.  Animals such as <em>C. elegans</em> possess many of these transporters. Humans have at least two closely related isoforms of the UDP-galactose:UMP exchange transporter.</p>
<p>NSTs generally appear to function by antiport mechanisms, exchanging a nucleotide-sugar for a nucleotide.   Thus, CMP-sialic acid is exchanged for CMP; GDP-mannose is preferentially exchanged for GMP, and UDP-galactose and UDP-N-acetylglucosamine are exchanged for UMP (or possibly UDP). Other nucleotide sugars (e.g., GDP-fucose, UDP-xylose, UDP-glucose, UDP-N-acetylgalactosamine, etc.) may also be transported in exchange for various nucleotides, but their transporters have not been molecularly characterized.  Each compound appears to be translocated by its own transport protein.  Transport allows the compound, synthesized in the cytoplasm, to be exported to the lumen of the Golgi apparatus or the endoplasmic reticulum where it is used for the synthesis of glycoproteins and glycolipids.  Comparable transport proteins exist for ATP which phosphorylates proteins, and phosphoadenosine phosphosulfate (PAPS) which is used as a percursor for protein sulfation.  It is not known if these transport proteins are members of the DMT superfamily.</p>
<p>The sequenced NSTs are generally of about 320-340 amino acyl residues in length and exhibit 8-12 putative transmembrane &alpha;-helical spanners.  An 8 TMS model has been presented by Kawakita et al. (<span id='ref5656.9562605' class='cite'>1998</span>) for the human UDP galactose transporter 1.</p>
<p>The generalized reaction catalyzed by NSTs is:</p>
<p align='center'>nucleotide-sugar (cytoplasm) + nucleotide (lumen) ⇌&nbsp;nucleotide-sugar (lumen) + nucleotide (cytoplasm)</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>2.A.7.13 The GDP-Mannose:GMP Antiporter (GMA) Family</strong></p>
<p>The yeast VRG4 protein, also called 'vanidate resistance protein', is a GDP-mannose transporter with the same size and topology as the other NSTs, but it shows very little sequence similarity with them. Only with the PSI-BLAST program with one iteration do these proteins exhibit apparent similarity. VRG4 is most similar to proteins in <em>C. elegans, Leishmania donovani, Arabidopsis thaliana,</em> and another <em>S. cerevisiae</em> protein reported to be of 249 aas (spP40027).</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>2.A.7.14 The Plant Organocation Permease (POP) Family</strong></p>
<p>A single member of the POP family (AtPUP1) has been functionally characterized.  It has been shown to transport adenine and cytosine with high affinity.  Evidence concerning energy coupling suggested an H<sup>+</sup> symport mechanism.  Purine derivatives (e.g., hypoxanthine), phytohormones (e.g., zeatin and kinetin) and alkaloids (e.g., caffeine) proved to be competitive inhibitors suggesting that they may be transport substrates. In fact trans-zeatin (a cytokinin) has been shown to be taken up, probably by at least two systems (<span id='ref6509.18835995' class='cite'>Cedzich <em>et al.</em> 2008</span>). The order of inhibition of adenine uptake by a variety of purine derivatives, phytohormones and alkaloids was reported to be: adenine, kinetin, caffeine, cytosine, zeatin, hypoxanthine, cytidine, nicotine, kinetin riboside, adenosine, zeatin riboside and thymine (<span id='ref5616.11337412' class='cite'>Williams and Miller, 2001</span>).  At least 15 members of this family have been sequenced from <em>A. thaliana </em>(<span id='ref5612.10662864' class='cite'>Gillissen <em>et al.</em>, 2000</span>)<em>.</em> Thus, AtPUP1 may be a broad specificity organocation transporter.  Other family members have been reported to exhibit different affinities for nucleobases.</p>
<p>The generalized transport reaction probably catalyzed by AtPUP1 is:</p>
<p align='center'>Organocation (out) + H<sup>+</sup><span style='font-family: times;'> (out)  &rarr; </span> Organocation (in) + H<sup>+</sup><span style='font-family: times;'> (in)</span></p>
<hr />
<p><span style='font-family: times;'><strong>2.A.7.15 The UDP-glucuronate/UDP-N-acetylgalactosamine Transporter (UGnT) Family</strong></span></p>
<hr />
<p><span style='font-family: times;'><strong>2.A.7.16 The GDP-fucose Transporter (GFT) Family</strong></span></p>
<hr />
<p><span style='font-family: times;'><strong>2.A.7.17 The Aromatic Amino Acid/Paraquat Exporter (ArAA/P-E) Family</strong></span></p>
<p><span style='font-family: times;'> The ArAA/PE family is a small family of proteobacterial proteins with 10 putative TMSs and sizes and sequences that most resemble the proteins of the DME family (2.A.7.3) within the DMT superfamily.  One member of this family, YddG of <em>E. coli</em> and <em>Salmonella typhimurium</em> (&lt;95% identical), have been functionally characterized (<span id='ref5622.12410826' class='cite'>Santiviago <em>et al</em>., 2002</span>; <span id='ref5217.17784858' class='cite'>Doroshenko <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>).  They are efflux pumps for paraquat (methyl viologen) which is a hydrophilic, doubly charged, quaternary ammonium compound that can participate in a redox cycle that generates oxygen free radicals in the bacterial cell under aerobic conditions.  YddG cannot pump out acriflavin, showing that it is fairly specific. It also exports aromatic amino acids. Therefore, it may not be a multidrug resistance pump.  Paraquat resistance is also dependent on the major <em>Salmonella</em> porin, OmpD.  Thus, YddG and OmpD are believed to function together in exporting paraquat to the external medium, but it is not known if this occurs in one or two steps (<span id='ref5622.12410826' class='cite'>Santiviago <em>et al</em>., 2002</span>). </span></p>
<p><span style='font-family: times;'> The overall reaction catalyzed by YddG is: </span></p>
<p align='center'><span style='font-family: times;'> Paraquat (in)   &rarr; </span> Paraquat (out).</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>2.A.7.18 The Choline Uptake Transporter (LicB-T) Family</strong></p>
<p>A single functionally characterized secondary transporter, LicB of <em>Haemophilus influenzae</em> defines the LicB-T family (Fan et al., 2003).  It has 292 aas and 10 putative TMSs.</p>
<p>LicB is a high-affinity choline permease that takes up choline under choline-limiting conditions.  It is required for the use of exogenous choline for the synthesis of phosphorylcholine which is incorporated into the bacterium's lipopolysaccharide (LPS).  It does not play a role in osmoprotection.  Phosphorylcholine derivatized LPS contributes to <em>H. influenzae's</em> pathogenesis by mimicry of host cell molecules (Fan et al., 2003).</p>
<p>The overall reaction catalyzed by LicB is probably:</p>
<p align='center'>choline (out) + H<sup>+</sup> (out) &rarr; choline (in) + H<sup>+</sup> (in).</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>2.A.7.19 The Nucleobase Uptake Transporter (NBUT) Family</strong></p>
<p>The allantoin permeases of <em>Phaseolus vulgaris</em> (French bean) and <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> have been shown to transport uracil and fluorouracil as well as allantoin (Schmidt et al., 2004).  <em>Arabidopsis</em> has several paralogues.  Distant homologues are present in <em>Bacteroides thetaiotamicron</em> (AAO77915) and <em>Entamoeba histolyticia</em> (EAL46705).  These proteins have 10 putative TMSs and comprise a distinct family in the DMT superfamily.</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>2.A.7.20 The Chloroquine Resistance Transporter (PfCRT) Family</strong></p>
<p>The <em>Plasmodium falciparum</em> chloroquine resistance protein (PfCRT) is a transporter as are its homologues in various species. In <em>Plasmodium</em> species it is localized to the intra-erythrocytic digestive vacuole. Mutations in this protein confer Verapamil-reversible chloroquine resistance to <em>P. falciparum.</em> The mutations in PfCRT give rise to increased compartment acidification. PfCRT-related changes in chloroquine response involve altered drug flux across the parasite degestive vacuole membrane.  It has been concluded that PfCRT directly mediates efflux of chloroquine from the digrestive vacuole (<span id='ref7809.15813727' class='cite'>Bray <em>et al.</em>, 2005</span>).</p>
<p>PfCRT is a 423 amino acyl protein with 10 putative TMSs, it probably catalyzes chloroquine quinine flux with H<sup>+</sup> across the digestive vacuole membrane (<span id='ref6190.12364789' class='cite'>Wellems, 2002</span>).  It is a member of the drug metabolite transporter (DMT) superfamily (TC #2.A.7) (<span id='ref6191.14702390' class='cite'>Tran and Saier, 2004</span>).  In frog oocytes it has been reported to activate various endogenous transporters (<span id='ref7807.15258157' class='cite'>Nessler <em>et al.</em>, 2004</span>). It transports a variety of qunoline drugs including quinine and quinidine. Mutations in TMSs 1, 4 and 9 alter drug specificity and determine levels of accumulation, suggesting that these TMSs play a role in substrate binding (<span id='ref7808.17163969' class='cite'>Cooper <em>et al.</em>, 2007</span>). Chloroquine-resistance reversers are substrates for mutant PfCRTs (<span id='ref7806.20598081' class='cite'>Lehane and Kirk, 2010</span>).</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>2.A.7.21 The 5 TMS Bacterial/Archaeal Transporter-2 (BAT2) Family</strong></p>
<p>The BAT2 family consists of 5 TMS proteins that resemble BAT family (2.A.7.2) proteins in size and topology, but show almost no sequence similarity with them.</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>2.A.7.22 The 4 TMS Small Multidrug Resistance-2 (SMR2) Family</strong></p>
<p>The SMR2 family consists of 4 TMS proteins, most about 110-130 aas long, but some longer, that resemble the SMR family (2.A.7.1) proteins in size and topology.  However, they show almost no sequence similarity.  Not all of them have the conserved glutamate in TMS1.  All close members of this family are from bacteria, but one distant member from <em>Neurospora crassa</em> has this domain N-terminal, fused to a CysT flavodoxin domain followed by a C-terminal radical SAM domain (<span id='ref6192.15289575' class='cite'>Nicolet and Drennan, 2004</span>).  This protein (gi85104851) is reported to be 1061 aas long.  Because this is the only protein in the database with this combination of fused domains, it could be artifactual.  Another homologue from <em>Frankia alni</em> (419 aas; gi111220000) has a putative 9 TMS topology with a C-terminal 300 residue hydrophilic domain.  Another protein, the TibA precursor glycoprotein adhesin/invasin of <em>E. coli</em> (336 aas; gi72166756) has 8 or 9 putative TMSs plus a C-terminal hydrophilic domain of nearly 100 residues.  It may be distantly related to members of the DME family (2.A.7.3).</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>2.A.7.23 The Putative Tryptophan Efflux (Trp-E) Family</strong></p>
<p>Expression of the <em>Bacillus subtilis</em> tryptophan biosynthetic genes <em>trpEDCFBA</em> and <em>trpG</em>, as well as a putative tryptophan transport gene (<em>trpP</em>), are regulated in response to tryptophan by the trp RNA-binding attenuation protein (TRAP). TRAP regulates expression of these genes by transcription attenuation and translation control mechanisms. TRAP and tryptophan also regulate translation of ycbK, a gene that encodes a protein of 312 aas and 10 TMSs, distantly related to members of the DMT superfamily (Yakhnin et al., 2006).</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>2.A.7.24 The Thiamine Pyrophosphate Transporter (TPPT) Family</strong></p>
<p>This family includes a diverse group of proteins from all types of eukaryotes as well as prokaryotes. The only one with an assigned probable function is the Thi74 protein of yeast. These proteins have 10 TMSs in a 2 + 8 arrangement (possibly 2 + 4 + 4). No mechanistic details of the transport process are available.</p>
<p>The reaction believed to be catalyzed by Thi74 is:</p>
<p align='center'>TPP (out) &rarr; TPP (in).</p>
<hr />
<p style='text-align: left;'><strong>2.A.7.25 The NIPA Mg2+ Uptake Permease (NIPA) Family</strong><br /><br />Mutations in the NIPA1(SPG6) gene of man, named for 'nonimprinted in Prader-Willi/Angelman' has been implicated in one form of autosomal dominant hereditary spastic paraplegia (HSP), a neurodegenerative disorder characterized by progressive lower limb spasticity and weakness. HSP comprises more than 30 genetic disorders whose predominant feature is a spastic gait. Mutations in at least six genes have been associated with autosomal dominant HSP including NIPA1(SPG6).<br /><br />Reduced magnesium concentration enhances expression of NIPA1 suggesting a role in cellular magnesium metabolism. Indeed, NIPA1 mediates Mg2+ uptake that is electrogenic, voltage-dependent, and saturable with a Michaelis constant of 0.69 &plusmn; 0.21 mM when expressed in Xenopus oocytes (<span id='ref6508.17166836' class='cite'>Goytain <em>et al.</em> 2007</span>). Subcellular localization with immunofluorescence showed that endogenous NIPA1 protein associates with early endosomes and the cell surface in a variety of neuronal and epithelial cells. As expected of a magnesium-responsive gene, altered magnesium concentration leads to a redistribution between the endosomal compartment and the plasma membrane; high magnesium results in diminished cell surface NIPA1 whereas low magnesium leads to accumulation in early endosomes and recruitment to the plasma membrane. The mouse NIPA1 mutants, T39R and G100R, corresponding to the respective human mutants, showed a loss of function when expressed in oocytes and altered trafficking in transfected COS7 cells. NIPA1 seems to encode a Mg2+ transporter, and the loss of function of NIPA1(SPG6) due to abnormal trafficking of the mutated protein provides the basis of the HSP phenotype (<span id='ref6508.17166836' class='cite'>Goytain <em>et al.</em> 2007</span>).<br /><br />NIPA has nine putative TMSs. Its mechanism of action is not known. It could be a channel or a carrier, and its energy dependency has not been studied. Homologues are found in a wide variety of animals, plants, and fungi. However, this family is clearly a member of the DMT superfamily (M. H. Saier, unpublished results).<br /><br />The transport reaction catalyzed by NIPA is:</p>
<p style='text-align: center;'>Mg<sup>2<sup>+</sup></sup> (out) &rarr; Mg<sup>2<sup>+</sup></sup> (in)</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>2.A.7.26 The 4 TMS Small Multidrug Resistance-3 (SMR3) Family</strong></p>
<p><span style='font-family: Times;'> YnfA is a 108 aa <em>E. coli</em> protein 
with 4 established TMSs and both the N- and C-termini in the periplasm (<span id='ref7020.11867724' class='cite'>Drew <em>et al.</em>, 2002</span>). Its homologues are found in a broad range of Gram-negative and 
Gram-positive bacteria as well as archaea and eukaryotes.  The sizes of bacterial 
homologues range from 98 aas to 132 aas, with a few exceptions. Plant proteins can be as large as 197aas.  The 
first two TMSs are homologous to the second two in these 4 TMS proteins.
  A <em>Methanosarciniae mazei</em> homologue of 94 aas and a <em>Geobacillus
 kaustophilus</em> homologue of 104 aas have only 2 TMSs with 30 residue 
extensions C- and N-terminal, respectively. No functional data are 
available for any of its homologues.  This family is the YnfA UPF0060 
family.&nbsp;</span></p>

ethidium bromide, acriflavine, safranin O and pyronin Y) (<span id="ref34546329.23165464" class="cite">Rodrigues <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Mmr of <em>Mycobacterium tuberculosis</em> (P69926)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.7.1.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.7.1.3&tcname=">2.A.7.1.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Small cationic multidrug efflux pump (substrates: cationic lipophilic drugs), EmrE. This pump confers resistance to a wide range of disinfectants and dyes known as quaternary cation compounds (QCCs). The 3-D structure of the dimeric EmrE shows opposite orientation of the two subunits in the membrane (<span>Chen <em>et al.</em>, 2007</span>), and this conclusion has been confirmed (<span>Fleishman <em>et al.</em> 2006</span>; <span>Lehner <em>et al.</em> 2008</span>; <span>Lloris-Garcer&aacute; <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>). There may be a single intermediate state in which the substrate is occluded and immobile (<span>Basting <em>et al</em>., 2008</span>).&nbsp; Direct interaction between substrates (tetraphenylphosphonium, TPP<sup>+</sup> and MTP<sup>+</sup>) and Glu14 in TMS1 has been demonstrated using solid state NMR (<span>Ong <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>).  A Gly<sub>90</sub>X<sub>6</sub>Gly<sub>97</sub> motif is important for dimer formation (<span>Elbaz <em>et al</em>., 2008</span>).&nbsp; Two models may account for the opposite (inverted) orientations of the two identical subunits. A post-translational model posits that topology remains malleable after synthesis and becomes fixed once the dimer forms. A second, co-translational model, posits that the protein inserts in both topologies in equal proportions (<span>Woodall <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; Protonation of E14 leads to rotation and tilt of transmembrane helices 1-3 in conjunction with repacking of loops, conformational changes that alter the coordination of the bound substrate and modulate its access to the binding site from the lipid bilayer. The transport model that emerges posits a proton-bound, but occluded, resting state. Substrate binding from the inner leaflet of the bilayer releases the protons and triggers alternating access between inward- and outward-facing conformations of the substrate-loaded transporter, thus enabling antiport without dissipation of the proton gradient (<span>Dastvan <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). TMS4 is the known dimerization domain of EmrE (<span>Julius <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Few conserved residues are essential for drug polyselectivity. Aromatic QCC selection involves a greater portion of conserved residues compared to other QCCs (<span>Saleh <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).<br />&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The topologies of helical membrane proteins are generally defined during insertion of the transmembrane
helices, yet topology can change after insertion. In EmrE, topology flipping occurs so that the populations in both orientations equalize. <span>Woodall <em>et al.</em> 2017</span> demonstrated that when EmrE is forced to insert in a distorted topology, topology
flipping of the first TMS can occur, and
topological malleability also extends to the C-terminal helix; even complete
inversion of the entire EmrE protein can occur after the full protein is translated and inserted.
Thus, topological rearrangements appear to be possible during biogenesis. Subtle but significant differences in the sizes of EmrE with different QCC ligands bound has been reported (<span>Qazi and Turner 2018</span>). The two Glu14 residues in the dimer have independent pKa values and are not electrostatically coupled (<span id="ref34554223.34607959" class="cite">Li <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). High level cell-free expression and specific labeling of EmrE has been achieved (<span id="ref34554618.14728684" class="cite">Klammt <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>). Cotranslational folding and assembly of the dimeric <em>E. coli</em> EmrE has been documented (<span id="ref34555476.35994672" class="cite">Mermans <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Harmane binding can uncoupled proton flux through EmrE. In <em>E. coli</em>, EmrE-mediated dissipation of the transmembrane pH gradient provides the mechanism underlying the in vivo phenotype of harmane susceptibility (<span id="ref34557476.36496486" class="cite">Spreacker <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>EmrE of <em>E. coli</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.7.1.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.7.1.4&tcname=">2.A.7.1.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Quaternary ammonium compound (cetylpyridinium, cetyldimethyl ethylammonium, hexadecyltrimethyl ammonium) efflux pump, SugE, of 105 aas and 4 TMSs.&nbsp; High level cell-free expression and specific labeling of EmrE has been achieved (<span id="ref34554618.14728684" class="cite">Klammt <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SugE of <em>E. coli</em> (P69937)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.7.1.5"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.7.1.5&tcname=">2.A.7.1.5</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>The heterooligomeric drug resistance efflux pump, YkkCD (substrates: ethidium bromide, proflavin, tetraphenylarsonium  chloride, crystal violet, pyronin Y, methylviologen, cetylperdinium chloride, streptomycin, tetracycline, chloramphenicol, phosphonomycin)</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Bacteria</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>YkkCD of <I>Bacillus subtilis</I> </TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.7.1.6"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.7.1.6&tcname=">2.A.7.1.6</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>The heterooligomeric drug resistance efflux pump, EbrAB (substrates: ethidium bromide, acriflavin, pyronin Y, and safranin O) (<span id="ref5657.17417881" class="cite">Zhang <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>).</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Bacteria</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>EbrAB of <I>Bacillus subtilis</I> </TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.7.1.7"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.7.1.7&tcname=">2.A.7.1.7</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The drug resistance efflux pump, Hsmr (<span id="ref5623.12551892" class="cite">Ninio and Schuldiner, 2003</span>) (exports ethidium, acriflavin tetraphenylphosphonium (TPP) and other cationic drugs).&nbsp; SLC35B4 plays a role in 
hepatocellular carcinoma (HCC) tumorigenesis (<span id="ref34555351.35308201" class="cite">Jiang <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). The expression levels of SLC35B4 are higher in HCC tissues than 
adjacent non-tumor tissues, and SLC35B4 is important for the proliferation 
and tumorigenesis of HCC cells. Mechanistically, SLC35B4 is important 
for the O-GlcNAc modification of c-Myc and thus the stabilization of

The apparent affinities of SLC35B1 for ATP/ADP on the internal face were approximately 13 times higher than those on the external side (<span id="ref34554384.35041824" class="cite">Schwarzbaum <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). ATP/ADP exchange by SLC35B1 is not strict; other di- and trinucleotides can act as counter-substrates for ATP, although mononucleotides and nucleotide sugars were not transported. Conserved residues K117 and K120 in TMS 4 and K277 in TMS 9 play critical roles in transport. The fact that SLC35B1 can promote ATP transport in exchange for ADP or UDP suggests a more direct coupling between ATP import requirements and the need for eliminating ADP and UDP, which are generated as side products of reactions taking place in the ER-lumen (<span id="ref34554384.35041824" class="cite">Schwarzbaum <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). SLC35B1 contributes to the 
uptake of UDPGA into the endoplasmic reticulum for glucuronidation

Both of these transporters seem to play a role in the apoptotic response induced by VSV (<span id="ref34550014.31320712" class="cite">Moskovskich <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Inherited thrombocytopenia can be caused by variants in crucial genes for glycosylation including that for SLC35A1 (<span id="ref34556183.36982178" class="cite">Mar&iacute;n-Qu&iacute;lez <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SLC35A1 of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.7.12.12"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.7.12.12&tcname=">2.A.7.12.12</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Putative Golgi UDP-sugar transporter, SLC35A4. UDP-galactose (Gal). 
These nucleotide sugars are competitive, alternate, noncooperative 
substrates. Mutant <span class="highlight">sqv-7</span> missense alleles result in severe reductions of these three transport activities. <span class="highlight">SQV-7</span> did not transport CMP-sialic acid, GDP-fucose, UDP-N-acetylglucosamine, UDP-glucose, or GDP-mannose (<span id="ref5613.11259660" class="cite">Berninsone <em>et al.</em> 2001</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SQV-7 (yk46f1.5) of <em>Caenorhabditis elegans</em> (Q18779)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.7.15.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.7.15.3&tcname=">2.A.7.15.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Endoplasmic reticulum (ER)/Golgi antiporter for UDP-glucuronic acid, UDP-N-acetylglucosamine and possibly UDP-xylose in exchange for UDP, Fringe connection (Frc) Essential for several signalling pathways including heparan sulfate and Fringe-dependent signalling (<span id="ref11079.11533660" class="cite">Selva <em>et al.</em> 2001</span>).&nbsp; Involved in glycosylation and processing of Notch (<span id="ref11075.11533661" class="cite">Goto <em>et al.</em> 2001</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Frc of <em>Drosophila melanogaster</em><br /> (Q95YI5)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.7.15.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.7.15.4&tcname=">2.A.7.15.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The UDP glucuronate/UDP-N-acetylgalactosamine transporter, Slc35D1; responsible for Schneckenbecken dysplasia in humans (<span id="ref4529.17952091" class="cite">Hiraoka <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>). Congenital disorders of glycosylation (CDGs) are a heterogeneous group 
of disorders with impaired glycosylation of proteins and lipids. These 
conditions have multisystemic clinical manifestations, resulting in 
gradually progressive complications including skeletal involvement and 
reduced bone mineral density (<span id="ref34555346.34441372" class="cite">Lipiński <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SLC35D1 of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.7.15.5"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.7.15.5&tcname=">2.A.7.15.5</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The putative Golgi nucleotide-sugar transporter SLC35D3 (416aas, 10 TMSs) (<span>Chintala <em>et al.</em> 2007</span>).&nbsp; SLC35D3 regulates tissue-specific autophagy and plays an important role in the increased autophagic activity required for the survival of subsets of dopaminergic neurons (<span>Wei <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). <span class="highlight">SLC35D3</span> silenced cells show increased expression of genes related to fat 
synthesis, and increased deposition of intramuscular fat,&nbsp; an
abundance of lipid droplets, and an increased level of free fatty acid in 
the culture medium. In contrast, the siRNA decreased the expression of 
genes involved in fat catabolism (<span id="ref34551057.32087688" class="cite">Li <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>), suggesting a role in the transport of fatty acids or their derivatives. It may play a role during porcine intramuscular preadipocyte differentiation (<span id="ref34551057.32087688" class="cite">Li <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SLC35D3 of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><BR><BR></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD COLSPAN=5><b><A HREF="tccluster.php?cluster=2.A.7.16">2.A.7.16 The GDP-fucose Transporter (GFT) Family</A></b></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff"><TD COLSPAN=5><p><BR></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.7.16.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.7.16.1&tcname=">2.A.7.16.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The GDP-fucose transporter (GFT) (defective in human leukocyte adhesion disease II) (SLC35C1) (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.7#ref10271">Zhang <em>et al.</em> 2012</a>).&nbsp; SLC35A1 and SLC35C1, have been related to congenital disorder of glycosylation II (CDG II) (<span id="ref10647.23506892" class="cite">Song 2013</span>). Triclosan activates c-Jun/miR-218-1-3p/SLC35C1
 signaling to regulate the cell viability, migration, invasion and 
inflammatory response of trophoblast cells (<span id="ref34555342.35668364" class="cite">Huo <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Elevated global fucosylation characterizes human intrahepatic cholangiocarcinoma, contributing to cell growth and migration via the upregulation of the NOTCH and EGFR/NF-kB pathways (<span id="ref34556037.36789652" class="cite">Ament <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). SLC35C1 knockdown significantly inhibited the proliferation, migration 
and invasive ability of glioma cells, while SLC35C1 overexpression 
promoted proliferation, migration, invasion and colony formation of 
glioma cells (<span id="ref34556203.37005450" class="cite">Xie <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp;</p>

<span>Proton-coupled transporter that transports a
 wide spectrum of oxo derivatives of heterocyclic nitrogen compounds, 
including allantoin, uric acid and xanthine, but not adenine. UPS5 mediates 
transport of uracil and 5-fluorouracil (a toxic uracil analog) (<span id="ref34545629.16738859" class="cite">Schmidt <em>et al.</em> 2006</span>).&nbsp; Allantoin accumulation mediated by UPS5 confers salt stress tolerance (<span id="ref34545630.27209043" class="cite">Lescano <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). </span>AtUPS5-Long and AtUPS5-Short localization indicates that they play different roles in allantoin distribution between subcellular compartments. Thus,&nbsp;<span id="ref34550644.31862838" class="cite">Lescano <em>et al.</em> 2019</span> predict that under non-stress conditions UPS5L and UPS5S may function in allantoin degradation pathway for nutrient recycling, whereas under stress conditions, both splice variants are involved in cell export via vesicles of the secretory pathway, allowing allantoin translocation from roots to shoots.</p>

COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.7.20.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.7.20.1&tcname=">2.A.7.20.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Chloroquine resistance transporter, PfCRT. Martin et al. (<span>2009</span>) have demonstrated Chloroquine transport via PfCRT. It co-transports chloroquine (CQ) or piperaquine (PPQ) and H<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>out of the digestive vacuole (and hence away from its site of action) via a mutant form of the parasite's CRT (<span>Lehane and Kirk, 2010</span>).&nbsp; Many mutations give rise to resistance (<span>Tan <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>; <span id="ref34548727.30141388" class="cite">Buppan <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). The orthologue in <em>P. vivax </em>is 73% identical to the <em>P. faciparum</em> protein and has the same function (<span>S&aacute; <em>et al.</em> 2006</span>). It is inhibited by verapamil, quinine, saquinavir and dibemethin 6a (<span>Meier <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). TMS1 is involved in substrate selectivity and catalyzes chroroquine efflux (<span id="ref34548950.30308208" class="cite">Antony <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). The vacuolar-half and membrane-spanning domains (especially TMS9) of PfCRT are more conserved, suggesting that its physiological substrate is expelled out of the parasite digestive vacuole. In the PfCRT occluded state, some evolutionary conserved sites, including positions related to drug resistance mutations, participate in a putative binding pocket located at the core of the PfCRT membrane-spanning domain (<span id="ref34551177.32179795" class="cite">Copp&eacute;e <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). pH-dependence of the <em>P. falciparum</em> chloroquine resistance transporter is linked to the transport cycle (<span id="ref34556878.37454114" class="cite">Berger <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp; Several proteases are localized to the digestive vacuole and these 
proteases sequentially breakdown hemoglobin into small peptides, 
dipeptides, and amino acids. The peptides are exported into the host 
cytoplasm via the chloroquine-resistance transporter and an amino acid 
transporter has also been identified on the digestive vacuole membrane (<span id="ref34557923.38535526" class="cite">Wiser 2024</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Protozoans</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>PfCRT of <em>Plasmodium falciparum</em> (AF495378)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.7.20.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.7.20.2&tcname=">2.A.7.20.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Crt homologue 1 (Chloroquine resistance transporter paralogue 1) (DdCRTp1)</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Amoeba</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Crtp1 of <em>Dictyostelium discoideum </em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.7.20.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.7.20.3&tcname=">2.A.7.20.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Uncharacterized protein of 384 aas and 11 TMSs</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>UP of <em>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii (Chlamydomonas smithii)</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.7.20.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.7.20.4&tcname=">2.A.7.20.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Chloroplastic chloroquine resistance transporter-1 of 447 aas and 10 TMSs, Clt-1.&nbsp; <span>Involved in thiol transport from the

Its homologues are found in a broad range of Gram-negative and Gram-positive bacteria as well as archaea and eukaryotes. The sizes of bacterial homologues range from 98 aas to 132 aas, with a few exceptions. Plant proteins can be as large as 197aas. The first two TMSs are homologous to the second two in these 4 TMS proteins. A <em>Methanosarciniae mazei</em> homologue of 94 aas and a <em>Geobacillus kaustophilus </em>homologue of 104 aas have only 2 TMSs with 30 residue extensions C- and N-terminal, respectively. No functional data are available for any of its homologues. This family is the YnfA UPF0060 family.</p><BR></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR Cells lacking Csg2p accumulate Ca<sup>2+&nbsp;</sup>in a pool which is exchangeable with extracellular Ca<sup>2+</sup> . The mutant cells are Ca<sup>2+</sup>&nbsp;sensitive. The protein has 410 amino acyl residues, with 9-10 TMSs. It exhibits an EF-hand Ca<sup>2+</sup>&nbsp;binding motif on the lumenal side of the endoplasmic reticular membrane. It is possible that it functions in Ca<sup>2+</sup><sup>&nbsp;</sup>sequestration. It regulates the activities of CSH1 and SUR1 during mannosyl phosphorylinositol ceramid synthesis. It forms heterodimers with CSH1 and SUR1 (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.7#ref9860">Beeler <em>et al.</em> 1994</a>; <a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.7#ref9861">Takita <em>et al.</em> 1995</a>). Cls2p likely functions in releasing Ca<sup>2+</sup>&nbsp;from the endoplasmic reticulum, somehow cooperating with calcineurin (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.7#ref9862">Tanida <em>et al.</em> 1996</a>).  It regulates the transport and protein leves of the inositol phosphorlyceramide mannosyltransferases Csg1 and Csh1 (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.7#ref9779">Uemura <em>et al.</em> 2007</a>).<span style="font-family: Times;"><br /></span></p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Yeast</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Csg2 of <em>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</em> COLSPAN=5><b><A HREF="tccluster.php?cluster=2.A.7.4">2.A.7.4 The Plant Amino Acid Transporter (UMAMIT) (formerly the P-DME) Family</A></b></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff"><TD COLSPAN=5><p><p><span style='caret-color: #000000; color: #000000;'>The P-DME (UMAMIT) family is a large subset of the DME family. All of these proteins are derived from plants, and they cluster loosely together on a phylogenetic tree that includes all members of the DME and P-DME families. All of these proteins are predicted by various methods to have 10 TMSs. If this suggestion proves to be correct, then the two halves of these proteins will have opposite orientation in the membrane. The P-DME family members have been called UMAMIT, and form large gene families in <em>Arabidopsis</em> (47 members) and rice (53 members). The few characterized members from <em>Arabidopsis </em>mediate amino acid export from the cytosol, with two of them shown to function as facilitators. They can be found in the plasma membrane (Ladwig et al 2012, Muller et al 2015, Besnard et al 2016, 2018) or the vacuolar membrane (Ranocha et al, 2010, Besnard et al 2018). They play multiple role in amino acid translocation between the organs of the plants, e.g. from leaves to seeds or to roots&rdquo;.</span></p><BR></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.7.4.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.7.4.1&tcname=">2.A.7.4.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>MtN21 nodulin protein</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Plants</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>MtN21 of <I>Medicago truncatula</I></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.7.4.10"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.7.4.10&tcname=">2.A.7.4.10</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>USUALLY MULTIPLE AMINO ACIDS MOVE IN AND OUT TRANSPORTER (UMAMIT) 
family proteiin, UMAMIT31 is a
glucosinolate exporters with a uniport mechanism. Loss-of-function 
umamit29 umamit30 umamit31 triple mutants have a very low level of seed 
glucosinolates, demonstrating a key role for these transporters in 
translocating glucosinolates into seeds. The
 UMAMIT uniporters facilitate glucosinolate efflux from biosynthetic 
cells along the electrochemical gradient into the apoplast, where the 
high-affinity H<sup>+</sup>-coupled glucosinolate importers GLUCOSINOLATE TRANSPORTERS (GTRs) load them into the phloem for translocation to the seeds (<a class="reflink" href="../../search/result.php?tc=2.A.7#ref34556304">Xu <em>et al.</em> 2023</a>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>UMAMIT31 of <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.7.4.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.7.4.2&tcname=">2.A.7.4.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Nodulin MfN21 or UMAMIT24 of 355 aas and 10 TMSs. UMAMIT24 and UMAMIT25 function in amino acid transfer in 
developing seeds. <span id="ref34551974.30312461" class="cite">Besnard <em>et al.</em> 2018</span> showed that UMAMIT24 and UMAMIT25 
promote export of a broad range of amino acids. They are expressed in distinct tissues within 
developing seeds; UMAMIT24 is mainly expressed in the chalazal seed coat
 and localized on the tonoplast, whereas the plasma membrane-localized 
UMAMIT25 is expressed in endosperm cells.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plants</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>MfN21 of <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> (NP_173898)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.7.4.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.7.4.3&tcname=">2.A.7.4.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Nodulin MtN21/EamA-like transporter</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plants</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Nodulin MtN21 of <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em>&nbsp;(Q9ZUI8)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.7.4.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.7.4.4&tcname=">2.A.7.4.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>WAT1 (Walls are thin 1) of 389 aas and 10 TMSs in a 5 + 5 TMS arrangement. <span>Required for secondary wall formation in fibers,

It is involved in phloem unloading in <em>Arabidopsis</em> roots (<span id="ref34551972.27856708" class="cite">Besnard <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). UMAMIT14 is expressed in root pericycle and phloem cells and 
mediates export of a broad range of amino acids. 
Loss-of-function of UMAMIT14 leads to a reduced shoot-to-root and 
root-to-medium transfer of amino acids originating from the leaves. 
These fluxes were further reduced in an umamti14 umamit18 double 
loss-of-function mutant. Thus, UMAMIT14 is involved 
in phloem unloading of amino acids in roots, and both UMAMIT14 and 
UMAMIT18 are involved in the radial transport of amino acids in roots (<span id="ref34551972.27856708" class="cite">Besnard <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>UMAMIT14 in <em>Arabidopsis thaliana </em>(Mouse-ear cress)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.7.4.6"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.7.4.6&tcname=">2.A.7.4.6</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Red1; UMAMIT18; WAT1-related protein At1g44800, or SIART of 370 aas and 10 TMSs. It is an L-amino acid efflux transporter that can function bidirectionally into and out 
of the cell (<span id="ref34551976.26628011" class="cite">M&uuml;ller <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). In <em>Arabidopsis</em>, SIAR1 localizes to the plasma 
membrane and is expressed in the vascular tissue, in the pericycle, stamen, and the chalazal seed coat of ovules and developing seeds. 
Mutant alleles of SIAR1 accumulate anthocyanins as a symptom of reduced 
amino acid content in the early stages of silique development (<span id="ref34551973.22312005" class="cite">Ladwig <em>et al.</em> 2012</span>).</p>

umamit29 umamit30 umamit31 triple mutants have a very low level of seed

TPT catalyses the strict 1:1 exchange of triose-phosphate, 3-phosphoglycerate and inorganic phosphate across the chloroplast envelope&nbsp;<span id="ref34547320.28970497" class="cite">Lee <em>et al.</em> 2017</span> reported crystal structures of TPT bound to two different substrates, 3-phosphoglycerate and inorganic phosphate, in occluded conformations. The structures reveal that TPT adopts a 10-transmembrane drug/metabolite transporter fold. Both substrates are bound within the same central pocket, where conserved lysine, arginine and tyrosine residues recognize the shared phosphate group. A structural comparison with the outward-open conformation of the bacterial drug/metabolite transporter suggests a rocker-switch motion of helix bundles, and molecular dynamics simulations support a model in which this rocker-switch motion is tightly coupled to substrate binding to ensure strict 1:1 exchange. The results reveal the mechanism of sugar phosphate/phosphate exchange by TPT. TPTexports&nbsp; Calvin cycle intermediates from chloroplasts and plays fundamental roles in nearly all photosynthetic eukaryotes (<span id="ref34547320.28970497" class="cite">Lee <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Red algae</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TPT <em>Galdieria sulphuraria </em>(B5AJT1)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.7.9.6"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.7.9.6&tcname=">2.A.7.9.6</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Chloroplast Glucose-6-P/P<sub>i </sub>antiporter-2, Gpt2</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plants</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Gpt2 of <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> (Q94B38)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.7.9.7"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.7.9.7&tcname=">2.A.7.9.7</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>solute carrier family 35, member E2B, SLC35A2B, of 405 aas and 8-10 TMSs.
